<meta name="DC.Title" content="De Novo Genome Assembly of the Economically Important Weed Horseweed Using Integrated Data from Multiple Sequencing Platforms" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1104/pp.114.247668" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2014-11-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2014 American Society of Plant Biologists. All Rights Reserved.." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Relation" content="10.1104/pp.114.900500" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Horseweed ( Conyza canadensis ), a member of the Compositae (Asteraceae) family, was the first broadleaf weed to evolve resistance to glyphosate. Horseweed, one of the most problematic weeds in the world, is a true diploid (2 n = 2 x = 18), with the smallest genome of any known agricultural weed (335 Mb). Thus, it is an appropriate candidate to help us understand the genetic and genomic bases of weediness. We undertook a draft de novo genome assembly of horseweed by combining data from multiple sequencing platforms (454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000, and PacBio RS) using various libraries with different insertion sizes (approximately 350 bp, 600 bp, 3 kb, and 10 kb) of a Tennessee-accessed, glyphosate-resistant horseweed biotype. From 116.3 Gb (approximately 350× coverage) of data, the genome was assembled into 13,966 scaffolds with 50% of the assembly = 33,561 bp. The assembly covered 92.3% of the genome, including the complete chloroplast genome (approximately 153 kb) and a nearly complete mitochondrial genome (approximately 450 kb in 120 scaffolds). The nuclear genome is composed of 44,592 protein-coding genes. Genome resequencing of seven additional horseweed biotypes was performed. These sequence data were assembled and used to analyze genome variation. Simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphisms were surveyed. Genomic patterns were detected that associated with glyphosate-resistant or -susceptible biotypes. The draft genome will be useful to better understand weediness and the evolution of herbicide resistance and to devise new management strategies. The genome will also be useful as another reference genome in the Compositae. To our knowledge, this article represents the first published draft genome of an agricultural weed.

*   ### Glossary
    
    N50
    :   50% of the assembly
    SSR
    :   simple sequence repeat
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    GST
    :   glutathione S -transferase
    GT
    :   glycosyltransferase
    ABC
    :   ATP-binding cassette
    SNP
    :   single-nucleotide polymorphism
    Kmer
    :   the possible subsequences of length k from reads obtained through DNA sequencing effort" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Horseweed (&lt;i&gt;Conyza canadensis&lt;/i&gt;), a member of the Compositae (Asteraceae) family, was the first broadleaf weed to evolve resistance to glyphosate. Horseweed, one of the most problematic weeds in the world, is a true diploid (2&lt;i&gt;n&lt;/i&gt; = 2&lt;i&gt;x&lt;/i&gt; = 18), with the smallest genome of any known agricultural weed (335 Mb). Thus, it is an appropriate candidate to help us understand the genetic and genomic bases of weediness. We undertook a draft de novo genome assembly of horseweed by combining data from multiple sequencing platforms (454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000, and PacBio RS) using various libraries with different insertion sizes (approximately 350 bp, 600 bp, 3 kb, and 10 kb) of a Tennessee-accessed, glyphosate-resistant horseweed biotype. From 116.3 Gb (approximately 350× coverage) of data, the genome was assembled into 13,966 scaffolds with 50% of the assembly = 33,561 bp. The assembly covered 92.3% of the genome, including the complete chloroplast genome (approximately 153 kb) and a nearly complete mitochondrial genome (approximately 450 kb in 120 scaffolds). The nuclear genome is composed of 44,592 protein-coding genes. Genome resequencing of seven additional horseweed biotypes was performed. These sequence data were assembled and used to analyze genome variation. Simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphisms were surveyed. Genomic patterns were detected that associated with glyphosate-resistant or -susceptible biotypes. The draft genome will be useful to better understand weediness and the evolution of herbicide resistance and to devise new management strategies. The genome will also be useful as another reference genome in the Compositae. To our knowledge, this article represents the first published draft genome of an agricultural weed.&lt;/p&gt;" />

Peng;citation_author=PJ. Tranel;citation_author=CN. Stewart;citation_author=JS. Yuan;citation_title=Novel software package for cross-platform transcriptome analysis (CPTRA);citation_pages=S16;citation_volume=10;citation_year=2009;citation_doi=10.1186/1471-2105-10-16" />
<meta name="citation_fulltext_world_readable" content="" />
<meta name="twitter:title" content="De Novo Genome Assembly of the Economically Important Weed Horseweed Using Integrated Data from Multiple Sequencing Platforms" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantphysiol.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantphysiol/166/3.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Horseweed ( Conyza canadensis ), a member of the Compositae (Asteraceae) family, was the first broadleaf weed to evolve resistance to glyphosate. Horseweed, one of the most problematic weeds in the world, is a true diploid (2 n = 2 x = 18), with the smallest genome of any known agricultural weed (335 Mb). Thus, it is an appropriate candidate to help us understand the genetic and genomic bases of weediness. We undertook a draft de novo genome assembly of horseweed by combining data from multiple sequencing platforms (454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000, and PacBio RS) using various libraries with different insertion sizes (approximately 350 bp, 600 bp, 3 kb, and 10 kb) of a Tennessee-accessed, glyphosate-resistant horseweed biotype. From 116.3 Gb (approximately 350× coverage) of data, the genome was assembled into 13,966 scaffolds with 50% of the assembly = 33,561 bp. The assembly covered 92.3% of the genome, including the complete chloroplast genome (approximately 153 kb) and a nearly complete mitochondrial genome (approximately 450 kb in 120 scaffolds). The nuclear genome is composed of 44,592 protein-coding genes. Genome resequencing of seven additional horseweed biotypes was performed. These sequence data were assembled and used to analyze genome variation. Simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphisms were surveyed. Genomic patterns were detected that associated with glyphosate-resistant or -susceptible biotypes. The draft genome will be useful to better understand weediness and the evolution of herbicide resistance and to devise new management strategies. The genome will also be useful as another reference genome in the Compositae. To our knowledge, this article represents the first published draft genome of an agricultural weed.

*   ### Glossary
    
    N50
    :   50% of the assembly
    SSR
    :   simple sequence repeat
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    GST
    :   glutathione S -transferase
    GT
    :   glycosyltransferase
    ABC
    :   ATP-binding cassette
    SNP
    :   single-nucleotide polymorphism
    Kmer
    :   the possible subsequences of length k from reads obtained through DNA sequencing effort" />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="46609" id="node46609--2" data-pisa="plantphysiol;166/3/1241" data-pisa-master="plantphysiol;pp.114.247668" data-apath="/plantphysiol/166/3/1241.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-jcore-article-title-compact clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-type highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span><span class="separator-pipe"></span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-art-cat highwire-cite-metadata"><span class="wrapper">RESEARCH ARTICLES - FOCUS ISSUE</span></span><span class="separator-pipe"></span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-art-series highwire-cite-metadata">F</span></div>
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-access"><span class="highwire-citation-access"><i class="highwire-access-icon highwire-access-icon-open-access open-access icon-unlock-alt" title="Open Access" aria-hidden="true"></i><span  class="element-invisible highwire-access-icon highwire-access-icon-open-access" style="">Open Access</span></span></div>
  
      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">De Novo Genome Assembly of the Economically Important Weed Horseweed Using Integrated Data from Multiple Sequencing Platforms</h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Yanhui Peng</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Zhao Lai</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Thomas Lane</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Madhugiri Nageswara-Rao</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Miki Okada</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Marie Jasieniuk</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="6">Henriette O’Geen</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Ryan W. Kim</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="8">R. Douglas Sammons</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="9">Loren H. Rieseberg</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="10">C. Neal Stewart</span></span></div>
  
  
  
  
</div>
<div id="hw-article-author-popups-node46609--2" style="display: none;"><div class="author-tooltip-0"><div class="author-tooltip-name">Yanhui Peng </div><div class="author-tooltip-affiliation"><span class="author-tooltip-text"><div class='author-affiliation'>Department of Plant Science, University of Tennessee, Knoxville, Tennessee 37996 (Y.P., T.L., M.N.-R., C.N.S.);</div></span></div><ul class="author-tooltip-find-more"><li class="author-tooltip-gs-link first"><a href="/lookup/google-scholar?link_type=googlescholar&amp;gs_type=author&amp;author%5B0%5D=Yanhui%2BPeng%2B" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on Google Scholar</a></li><li class="author-tooltip-pubmed-link"><a     <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >

    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-corrections" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="messages highwire-corrections highwire-correction-type-correction"><h3 class="highwire-corrections-title">This article has a correction. Please see:</h3><ul class="highwire-corrections-list"><li class="relation-15057 first last"><a href="/content/166/4/2218" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">CORRECTIONS - December 01, 2014</a></li></ul></div>  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2014 American Society of Plant Biologists. All Rights Reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">Horseweed (<em>Conyza canadensis</em>), a member of the Compositae (Asteraceae) family, was the first broadleaf weed to evolve resistance to glyphosate. Horseweed, one of the most problematic weeds in the world, is a true diploid (2<em>n</em> = 2<em>x</em> = 18), with the smallest genome of any known agricultural weed (335 Mb). Thus, it is an appropriate candidate to help us understand the genetic and genomic bases of weediness. We undertook a draft de novo genome assembly of horseweed by combining data from multiple sequencing platforms (454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000, and PacBio RS) using various libraries with different insertion sizes (approximately 350 bp, 600 bp, 3 kb, and 10 kb) of a Tennessee-accessed, glyphosate-resistant horseweed biotype. From 116.3 Gb (approximately 350× coverage) of data, the genome was assembled into 13,966 scaffolds with 50% of the assembly = 33,561 bp. The assembly covered 92.3% of the genome, including the complete chloroplast genome (approximately 153 kb) and a nearly complete mitochondrial genome (approximately 450 kb in 120 scaffolds). The nuclear genome is composed of 44,592 protein-coding genes. Genome resequencing of seven additional horseweed biotypes was performed. These sequence data were assembled and used to analyze genome variation. Simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphisms were surveyed. Genomic patterns were detected that associated with glyphosate-resistant or -susceptible biotypes. The draft genome will be useful to better understand weediness and the evolution of herbicide resistance and to devise new management strategies. The genome will also be useful as another reference genome in the Compositae. To our knowledge, this article represents the first published draft genome of an agricultural weed.</p></div><p id="p-14">In the past few years, genomic approaches have revolutionized plant biology. Complete or draft genome data are currently available for tens of plant species (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net">http://www.phytozome.net</a>). From the model plant Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>; <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, 2000</a>) to important crop plants such as rice (<em>Oryza sativa</em>; <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Goff et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">Yu et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">International Rice Genome Sequencing Project, 2005</a>), soybean (<em>Glycine max</em>; <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Schmutz et al., 2010</a>), maize (<em>Zea mays</em>; <a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Schnable et al., 2009</a>), and chickpea (<em>Cicer arietinum</em>; <a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Varshney et al., 2013</a>) to economic woody plants such as wine grape (<em>Vitis vinifera</em>; <a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Jaillon et al., 2007</a>) and poplar (<em>Populus trichocarpa</em>; <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Tuskan et al., 2006</a>), powerful complete genome data sets and tools allow the unprecedented ability to explore the genetics and genomics of plant form and function. Furthermore, genome sequences of additional plant species will soon be available from in-progress large-scale sequencing projects (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/PLANTS/PlantList.html">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/PLANTS/PlantList.html</a>).</p><p id="p-15">One critical group of plants that has been largely overlooked in the genomics revolution consists of economically significant agricultural weeds (<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Stewart, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>). Weeds cause about $36 billion annual damage in the United States alone (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Pimentel et al., 2000</a>). The cost is even higher if one includes weeds in pastures, golf courses, aquatic environments, etc. Unchecked, weeds effectively outcompete crops for resources and decrease the yield of food, feed, and fiber. Despite their profound economic significance, weedy plant genomics data are very scarce relative to other economically important plants.</p><p id="p-16">Weeds experienced surreptitious accidental domestication (<a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Warwick and Stewart, 2005</a>) and adaptation to changing agricultural environments to become among the world’s most successful plants. They are persistent, adaptable, stress tolerant, competitive, and capable of extreme levels of reproduction (e.g. some species can produce one million seeds per plant). Herbicide application has been the dominant weed-control measure in recent decades, but increasingly, the evolution of herbicide resistance is challenging this practice. More than 400 biotypes of weeds have evolved resistance to one or more of all the major groups of herbicides (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Heap, 2014</a>), among which resistance to glyphosate is currently of greatest concern. Horseweed (<em>Conyza canadensis</em>), which is in the Compositae (Asteraceae) family, was the first eudicot weedy species to evolve glyphosate resistance and is also one of the most widely distributed glyphosate-resistant species in the world (<a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">VanGessel, 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Heap, 2014</a>). Horseweed is a true diploid (2<em>n</em> = 18), with a self-fertilizing mating system and the smallest genome of any agricultural weed (335 Mb; <a id="xref-ref-69-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>). We sought to describe the reference genome of horseweed in order to enable genomic approaches to elucidate the mechanism of nontarget herbicide resistance and to gain a better understanding of the evolution and spread of herbicide resistance across agricultural landscapes. Weed genomics is critical to understand weed biology, and understanding weed biology is critical in weed management. To date, transcriptomes using next-generation sequencing of a few weedy species have been produced to aid the discovery of genes that contribute to herbicide resistance and other weediness traits, with most progress occurring in <em>Conyza</em> and <em>Amaranthus</em> spp. and <em>Lolium rigidum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Lee et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Peng et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Riggins et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Lai et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Gaines et al., 2014</a>).</p><p id="p-17">Horseweed shares many other weediness features with the world’s most damaging weeds. These features include a relatively short vegetative phase, indeterminate flowering, self-compatibility, high seed output, long-distance seed dispersal, competitiveness with crop plants, a deep root system, discontinuous dormancy, environmental plasticity, and allelopathy (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Basu et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Chao et al., 2005</a>). Also, horseweed is amenable to genetic transformation and regeneration (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Halfhill et al., 2007</a>), which facilitates overexpression or knockdown analysis of potential gene targets. Autogamous, a single horseweed plant may produce well over 200,000 seeds. The small seeds have pappi that enable wind and water dispersal (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Bhowmik and Bekech, 1993</a>; <a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Weaver 2001</a>) to sometimes very long distances (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">DeVlaming and Proctor, 1968</a>; <a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Weaver and McWilliams, 1980</a>; <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Andersen, 1993</a>; <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Dauer et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Shields et al., 2006</a>). Moreover, horseweed seeds are not dormant and can germinate immediately after maturity in the fall, spring, or midsummer (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Bhowmik and Bekech, 1993</a>; <a id="xref-ref-80-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Weaver, 2001</a>). Thus, horseweed is a bona fide weed with model plant qualities.</p><p id="p-18">The Compositae is the largest and most diverse plant family, with over 24,000 described species. Despite the evolutionary success and economic importance of plants in this family, a draft whole-genome publication for any member of the family or for any plants from closely related families is lacking. However, genomes for lettuce (<em>Lactuca sativa</em>; <a href="https://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu">https://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu</a>), sunflower (<em>Helianthus annuus</em>; <a href="http://www.sunflowergenome.org">http://www.sunflowergenome.org</a>), and several other members of the family are available, but de novo genome assembly can often be arduous in many cases when genomes are large and complex (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Kane et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Truco et al., 2013</a>). A draft sequence of a streamlined Compositae genome would greatly advance the genomics and genetics of economically, ecologically, and evolutionarily important plants within this important plant family.</p><p id="p-19">In this study, in order to further increase sequence resources for horseweed, we performed de novo genome sequencing and assembly of the Tennessee glyphosate-resistant horseweed biotype TN-R using multiple sequencing platforms, including 454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000 and PacBio RS. Using a modified strategy that has proven effective in vertebrates (Li et al., 2010a), date palm (<em>Phoenix dactylifera</em>; <a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Al-Dous et al., 2011</a>), and flax (<em>Linum usitatissimum</em>; <a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Wang et al., 2012</a>), the draft genome of horseweed was assembled into scaffolds. Genomes and transcriptomes of this and another seven horseweed biotypes were also sequenced, and the data were used to investigate genome variation and to facilitate molecular marker development and gene discovery. The primary goal of this project was to build substantial genomic resources for horseweed, which will subsequently be useful to elucidate the genomic basis of weediness traits.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2>RESULTS</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>De Novo Sequencing and Assembly</h3><p id="p-20">The reference genome source was DNA extracted from six individual plants of a single glyphosate-resistant biotype that was originally collected in 2002 from a soybean field in Jackson, TN (TN-R; <a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Mueller et al., 2003</a>). Comprehensive genome shotgun sequences were obtained using seven libraries with insert sizes that ranged from 350 bp to 10 kb and three sequencing platforms (454 GS-FLX, Illumina HiSeq 2000, and PacBio RS; <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table I</a>). Two plates of 454 GS-FLX runs on three independent libraries (approximately 600 bp) produced 2,238,807 raw reads with an average read length of approximately 390 bp, for a yield total of approximately 860 Mb data. Two lanes of HiSeq 2000 runs (2 × 100 bp) of two short paired-end libraries produced 786,389,990 raw reads, for a yield total of approximately 77 Gb data. One lane of a HiSeq 2000 run (2 × 100 bp) of one 3-kb mate-paired library produced 381,844,926 raw reads, for a yield total of approximately 37 Gb data. Ten SMRT cells of PacBio RS runs produced 513,084 sequences with an average read length of 3.1 kb, which yielded 1.44 Gb of total data (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table II</a>). All the sequence data together represented approximately 350× coverage of the horseweed genome.</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111815/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111815/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table I.</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary of sequencing read yield results from multiple platforms and mate-paired libraries of various sizes (approximately 350 bp, 600 bp, 3 kb, and 10 kb)</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111622/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111622/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table II.</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary of the de novo genome assembly of horseweed</span><p id="p-21" class="first-child">N<sub>90</sub> and N<sub>70</sub>, 90% and 70% of the assembly, respectively.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-22">De novo genome assembly is often not straightforward (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Baker, 2012</a>), in that many factors impact the integrity and accuracy of the draft assembly. To integrate data from multiple sequencing platforms, we used a de novo assembly strategy (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S1</a>). Factors that could impact the assembly quality were tested, including <a id="xref-def-8-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">Kmer</a> (the possible subsequences of length <em>k</em> from reads obtained through DNA sequencing effort) size, sequencing depth and coverage, and the hybrid assembly of multiplex data (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S2</a>). Increased coverage could increase assembly quality by increasing the 50% of the assembly (<a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">N<sub>50</sub></a>) value and assembled genome size and may also reduce the numbers of contigs. However, as the coverage reached 50×, the effect of this factor was limited. For a given coverage, lower <a id="xref-def-8-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">Kmer</a> size translated into increased sensitivity, but with more assembly errors, while higher <a id="xref-def-8-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">Kmer</a> size translated into highly specific, accurate, and reliable assembly (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S2</a>). Therefore, given adequate coverage, higher <a id="xref-def-8-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">Kmer</a> provided better assembly. To mitigate potential problems, we chose deep coverage (greater than 50×), high <a id="xref-def-8-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">Kmer</a> (more than 25 nucleotides) cutoff for accuracy, and multiple sequencing platforms and library types. Indeed, the data that were integrated among multiple Next Generation Sequencing platforms and libraries with different insert sizes increased the quality of the de novo assembly (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S2</a>). Newbler was used, since it was designed specifically for assembling sequence data generated by the 454 GS-FLX (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Margulies et al., 2005</a>), whereas SOAPdenovo is among the most popular tools to assemble Illumina short reads (Li et al., 2010b). Using the PacBio RS sequencing platform, reads of over 10 kb in length were produced. However, the higher error rate prevented the direct use of PacBio reads in assembly (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S3</a>). After error correction by mapping high-quality, but short, Illumina reads, the inclusion of the long reads from PacBio increased the quality of de novo genome assembly by using it for either the primary assembly or the guidance of scaffolding (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Figs. S3–S5</a>). The filtered and high-quality 454 GS-FLX single reads and high-coverage Illumina paired-end reads were combined to generate de novo contigs. Mate-pair libraries included true mates mixed with chimeras and also paired ends. To find those true mates with correct orientation and distance, the de novo-assembled contigs were used as reference for the mapping of mate-pair reads (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S1</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S6</a>). The total size of the assembled contigs was 311.3 Mb, in which <a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">N<sub>50</sub></a> was composed of 5,122 sequences of 20,764 bp or longer (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>; <a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table II</a>). The contigs were further assembled to 344.9-Mb scaffolds, in which the <a id="xref-def-1-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">N<sub>50</sub></a> increased to 33,561 bp (3,575 sequences). Without gaps, the assembly contained 92.3% genome coverage. The horseweed genome contained a 34.9% G/C base ratio, which was higher than sequenced woody eudicot genomes but equivalent to other eudicot genomes and much lower than monocot genomes (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S2</a>). The G/C content of coding regions was 38.6%, which was higher than that of the entire genome (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1D</a>). The assembled draft of horseweed genome data has been submitted to the National Center for Biotechnology Information with accession number SUB535309.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="De novo genome assembly of horseweed. A, Assembly units were sorted by descending length, and the cumulative DNA content was plotted as a function of the total number of assembly units. B, Length distributions of de novo-assembled contigs. C, G/C content distributions in horseweed computed over a bin size of approximately 500 bp. D, G/C content distribution of unique coding DNA sequences." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-849132570" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;De novo genome assembly of horseweed. A, Assembly units were sorted by descending length, and the cumulative DNA content was plotted as a function of the total number of assembly units. B, Length distributions of de novo-assembled contigs. C, G/C content distributions in horseweed computed over a bin size of approximately 500 bp. D, G/C content distribution of unique coding DNA sequences.&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="369"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 1." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="369"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/111746" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> <p id="p-23" class="first-child">De novo genome assembly of horseweed. A, Assembly units were sorted by descending length, and the cumulative DNA content was plotted as a function of the total number of assembly units. B, Length distributions of de novo-assembled contigs. C, G/C content distributions in horseweed computed over a bin size of approximately 500 bp. D, G/C content distribution of unique coding DNA sequences.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-24">To inspect the de novo assembly accuracy, multiple independent sources of horseweed DNA and complementary DNA sequences were used to align with the assembled genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-1" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table III</a>). These sequences included EST reads from Sanger sequencing, transcriptome shotgun reads from 454 and Illumina sequencing, and partial genomic reads, which were used for the genome assembly (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">Zhou et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-55-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Peng et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Yuan et al., 2010</a>; sample identifiers WSYE and NUSE from the One-Thousand Plant Transcriptomes project [<a href="http://www.onekp.com">www.onekp.com</a>]). Over 90% of sequences could be aligned to the genome. The mapping and BLAST varied among sequence reads. However, in the aligned sequences, 91.2% or more had over 90% coverage and identity. The nonperfect alignments indicated that EST reads could be used to further improve assembly accuracy and gene prediction.</p><div id="T3" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111723/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111723/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table III.</span> <span class="caption-title">Alignment of ESTs, transcriptome sequencing (RNAseq), and genomic shotgun sequences to scaffolds</span><p id="p-25" class="first-child">The threshold of alignment coverage and identity was 90% or greater.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of Repetitive Elements and Annotation of Protein-Coding Genes</h3><p id="p-26">By using the plant repeat database (RepeatMasker libraries 20140131) and RepeatMasker 4.0.5, a total of 233,521 loci (19.5 Mb) were identified to be various nucleotide repeat elements. These sequences represented 6.25% of the assembled genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-4-1" class="xref-table" href="#T4">Table IV</a>) and were composed of 172,825 simple sequence repeats (<a id="xref-def-2-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a>s), 28,494 low-complexity elements, 23,425 different types of retroelements, 5,424 small RNA structures, 2,283 DNA transposons, and 675 unclassified elements. Retroelements were the largest mobile elements found in the genome (2.61%), of which most were long terminal repeat-type retroelements (2.54%). Small RNAs contributed 0.61% to the genome, whereas identified DNA transposons represented just 0.16% of the genome.</p><div id="T4" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111843/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111843/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table IV.</span> <span class="caption-title">Repeat element analysis in the horseweed genome</span><p id="p-27" class="first-child">For elements, most repeats were fragmented by insertions or deletions and have been counted as one element.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-28">Masked <a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> loci were analyzed using the Simple Sequence Repeat Identification Tool (<a href="http://archive.gramene.org/db/markers/ssrtool">http://archive.gramene.org/db/markers/ssrtool</a>) with a threshold minimum of six repeats. The results included 51,892 loci (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S3</a>) containing 44,589 dimer (85.9%), 6,864 trimer (13.2%), 299 tetramer (0.58%), 60 pentamer (0.12%), and 80 hexamer (0.15%) motifs. The majority of these loci contained six to nine repeats (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S7</a>). Considering the position in scaffold, repeat number, and length, 1,316 loci were identified to have appropriate sequences to develop PCR primers, which could be used as <a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> markers (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Okada et al., 2013</a>).</p><p id="p-29">Using previous EST and transcriptome data as a starting point (<a id="xref-ref-88-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">Zhou et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-55-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Peng et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-85-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Yuan et al., 2010</a>), 44,592 horseweed gene models were predicted. A total of 25,439 unique transcripts were returned after removing redundancies. The longest gene (using coding sequence only and without tallying introns) was composed of 17,120 bp. It was annotated to encode a midasin-like protein. The mean and median transcript lengths were 2,210 and 1,830 bp, respectively. Over 95% of the transcripts were longer than 715 bp (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a>). The predicted gene sequences were further compared with the UniProt and National Center for Biotechnology Information nonredundant protein databases for assigning biological information. Using a threshold e value of 10<sup>−4</sup> or less, 79.6% of these (20,248 of 25,439) had at least one hit to the database. The majority of the alignments had an identity of 50% or greater (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>). Within the annotated genes, 18,406 returned hits at e values of 10<sup>−10</sup> or less. Moreover, most of the hits had only one high-scoring segment pair with the aligned genes (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2, B and D</a>). Overall, the majority of the predicted genes in the assembled horseweed genome could be aligned with hits in a known protein database with high identity and specificity, which indicated that most of these genes were likely protein-coding sequences.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Annotation of protein-coding transcripts. A, Distribution of lengths of predicted transcripts. B, Identity distribution of alignments with hits in the database. C, E value distribution of aligned hits. D, Distribution of high-scoring segment pairs (HSPs) from aligned hits." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-849132570" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;Annotation of protein-coding transcripts. A, Distribution of lengths of predicted transcripts. B, Identity distribution of alignments with hits in the database. C, E value distribution of aligned hits. D, Distribution of high-scoring segment pairs (HSPs) from aligned hits.&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="325"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 2." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="325"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/111739" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 2.</span> <p id="p-30" class="first-child">Annotation of protein-coding transcripts. A, Distribution of lengths of predicted transcripts. B, Identity distribution of alignments with hits in the database. C, E value distribution of aligned hits. D, Distribution of high-scoring segment pairs (HSPs) from aligned hits.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-31">Gene Ontology (<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a>) annotation resulted in 14,897 <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a>-annotated genes. The largest gene category with molecular function was kinase activity (1,780), following by nucleotide binding (1,740), transporter activity (1,031), and another 15 subgroups with 50 or more terms (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S8A</a>). Among genes annotated to various biological processes, the largest subgroup was stress-response genes (5,446), followed by cellular component organization-related genes (4,582), transport (3,799), and 27 additional groups with 50 or more terms (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S8B</a>). The potential cellular localization of the genes was predicted by assigning 17,878 cellular component <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms to various queries (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S8C</a>). Genes that encoded proteins with enzyme activity were further divided into six groups (transferases, hydrolases, oxidoreductases, ligases, lyases, and isomerases; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S8D</a>).</p><p id="p-32">Fisher’s exact test showed 10 <a id="xref-def-3-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms that were significantly overrepresented in horseweed compared with Arabidopsis (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). Enrichment analysis using the <a id="xref-def-3-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms as well as related gene families included vacuolar transport, photosynthetic acclimation, detoxification of nitrogenous compounds, response to UV-B light, chloroplast thylakoid lumen, protein targeting to chloroplasts, protein peptidyl-prolyl isomerization, peptidyl-prolyl cis-trans-isomerase activity, hydrolase activity (acting on carbon-nitrogen), glycolysis, and NAD(P)H dehydrogenase complex assembly. Four transporter subgroup <a id="xref-def-3-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms were overrepresented in the horseweed genome at the <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001 level: drug transmembrane transporters (GO:0015238), xanthine transmembrane transporters (GO:0042907), uracil transmembrane transporters (GO:0015210), and allantoin uptake transporters (GO:0005274; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S9</a>). However, these data are not sufficient to draw conclusions about biological function and the evolution of herbicide resistance; further gene expression analysis and other follow-on experiments need to be performed.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Differential GO term distribution by enrichment analysis of the horseweed and Arabidopsis genomes using the Fisher&#x2019;s exact test. The entire transcriptome in horseweed was set as the test data set, and the entire transcriptome in Arabidopsis was set as the reference data set (P &#x2264; 0.001). [See online article for color version of this figure.]" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-849132570" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Differential GO term distribution by enrichment analysis of the horseweed and Arabidopsis genomes using the Fisher&#x2019;s exact test. The entire transcriptome in horseweed was set as the test data set, and the entire transcriptome in Arabidopsis was set as the reference data set (&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &#x2264; 0.001). [See online article for color version of this figure.]&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="293"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="293"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/111607" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-33" class="first-child">Differential <a id="xref-def-3-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> term distribution by enrichment analysis of the horseweed and Arabidopsis genomes using the Fisher’s exact test. The entire transcriptome in horseweed was set as the test data set, and the entire transcriptome in Arabidopsis was set as the reference data set (<em>P</em> ≤ 0.001). [See online article for color version of this figure.]</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-34">Gene families that are commonly associated with nontarget herbicide resistance include cytochrome P450s, glutathione <em>S</em>-transferases (<a id="xref-def-4-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GST</a>s), glycosyltransferases (<a id="xref-def-5-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GT</a>s), and ATP-binding cassette (<a id="xref-def-6-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ABC</a>) transporters (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Yuan at al., 2007</a>). Relative to Arabidopsis, the horseweed genome has more members in each of these families (<a id="xref-table-wrap-5-1" class="xref-table" href="#T5">Table V</a>). There were 323 unique cytochrome P450 genes at 401 loci in horseweed, which represents a 26% increase over Arabidopsis. Also, 155 <a id="xref-def-6-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ABC</a> transporter genes at 213 loci were found in horseweed, which is 14% more than in Arabidopsis. Horseweed had 6% more <a id="xref-def-5-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GT</a>s than Arabidopsis. There were 54 horseweed <a id="xref-def-4-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GST</a>s, which is one more gene than is found in Arabidopsis (<a id="xref-table-wrap-5-2" class="xref-table" href="#T5">Table V</a>).</p><div id="T5" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111587/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111587/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table V.</span> <span class="caption-title">Analysis of gene families (cytochrome P450s, <a id="xref-def-4-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GST</a>s, <a id="xref-def-6-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ABC</a> transporters, and <a id="xref-def-5-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GT</a>s) that are potentially involved in nontarget glyphosate resistance in horseweed compared with those tallied for Arabidopsis</span><p id="p-35" class="first-child">Loci data refer to the total potential copy numbers in the gene family.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Plastid and Mitochondrial Genomes</h3><p id="p-36">Plastid genome sequences from 454 GS-FLX reads were isolated by searching against known plastid genome databases and then subjected to de novo assembly. The assembled plastid genomes were further mapped with accurate Illumina reads to fix homopolymer errors. The entire chloroplast genome (approximately 153 kb; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S10</a>) and most of the mitochondrial genome (approximately 450 kb in scaffolds) were obtained (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S4</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Data Sets S1 and S2</a>). The horseweed chloroplast genome contained two inverted repeats (24,936 bp each), a large single-copy fragment (84,634 bp), and a small single-copy fragment (18,063 bp). The orientation of the two inverted repeats was determined by PCR. A total of 95 protein-coding genes (88 unique), 39 tRNA genes (28 unique), and eight ribosomal RNA genes (four unique) were annotated (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S1</a>). These genes comprised 59.7% of the chloroplast genome. The remainder, the noncoding portion of the chloroplast genome, was composed of introns, intergenic spacers, and pseudogenes. The largest gene was the <em>ycf2</em> gene, which was 6,085 bp. The smallest gene was a 34-bp tRNA gene. The G/C content in the chloroplast genome was 37.2%. The chloroplast genome was nearly identical to that of lettuce and sunflower, except in the orientation of the two inverted repeat elements (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S11</a>). The lettuce chloroplast has reverse inverted repeat elements compared with horseweed and sunflower.</p><p id="p-37">The horseweed mitochondrial genome reads from 454 GS-FLX, Illumina paired-end, and true-mate pairs were parsed and assembled into 123 scaffolds (<a id="xref-def-1-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">N<sub>50</sub></a> = 10,057 bp), with the help of plant mitochondrial databases. The scaffold sizes ranged from 315 to 43,498 bp, for a total of 453,334 bp in the mitochondrial genome (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S12</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S2</a>), which was moderately sized compared with other plant mitochondrial genomes.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Resequencing and Genomic Variation Analysis</h3><p id="p-38">We resequenced four population pairs that were either glyphosate resistant (R) or susceptible (S) from the United States. The representative populations used for resequencing were from California (CA-R versus CA-S), Delaware (DE-R versus DE-S), Indiana (IN-R versus IN-S), and Tennessee (TN-R versus TN-S). Individual seedlings surviving a glyphosate application were used for eventual DNA donors, wherein DNA was pooled for sequencing (<a id="xref-table-wrap-6-1" class="xref-table" href="#T6">Table VI</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S13</a>). Within two HiSeq 2000 flow cell lanes, a total 55.6 Gb (170× coverage) of paired-end reads was produced, and each of the seven additional genomes was assembled using TN-R as the reference genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-6-2" class="xref-table" href="#T6">Table VI</a>). Interpopulation genome variation included multiple-nucleotide variation, which was defined as 4 bp or less, with equal numbers of nucleotides at each locus, single-nucleotide variation, insertions/deletions, and replacement (<a id="xref-table-wrap-7-1" class="xref-table" href="#T7">Table VII</a>). When a minimum cutoff value (20 times or greater coverage at the variant loci, 20% or greater frequency) was applied to avoid calling errors, the frequency of detected variants in the genome varied among biotypes, ranging from 0.75 to 1.59 counts per kb (mean of 1.01; <a id="xref-table-wrap-7-2" class="xref-table" href="#T7">Table VII</a>). Compared with the TN-R reference genome, CA-R had the fewest variants, followed by, in ascending order, IN-R, CA-S, IN-S, DE-R, TN-S, and DE-S. IN-R, IN-S, and TN-S had fewer variants relative to each other compared with the rest of the other biotypes. Compared with their partners (DE-S and CA-S, respectively), DE-R and CA-R had the fewest variants. Similarly, IN-R had fewer variants than IN-S (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S5</a>). The minimum average quality score for the position of each variant was 30 (one error in 1,000; <a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4A</a>), which ensured that the results were quite reliable. To determine whether a variant was homozygous, we applied a threshold of 90% probability and found that IN-R and CA-R had the highest frequency of heterozygosity (nearly 80%), whereas only 50% of variants were heterozygous in the DE-R, DE-S, and TN-S biotypes (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4B</a>). Most of the sequence variation among samples was composed of single-nucleotide variations/single-nucleotide polymorphisms (<a id="xref-def-7-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a>s) in all eight biotypes (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4C</a>). Fisher’s exact test showed that a total of 2,010 specific variants were found in all four glyphosate-resistant biotypes, which were distributed among 1,370 loci. Only 539 specific variants were found in all four glyphosate-susceptible biotypes, and these were located at 425 loci (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4D</a>). Since the physical map of the horseweed genome was not available, the location of each specific variant in the sorted scaffold arrays instead of in the genome was listed (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S14</a>). A genome-wide association study requires <a id="xref-def-7-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a> information from the genomes of many control and test individuals. Specific <a id="xref-def-7-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a>s in glyphosate-resistant and glyphosate-susceptible genomes were located in hotspots, which were defined as the ones with low <em>P</em> values (<em>P</em> ≤ 0.01), and might be associated with the phenotypes of glyphosate resistance (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S14</a>). However, these findings are not definitive, given the low sample sizes of biotypes analyzed.</p><div id="T6" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111419/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111419/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table VI.</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary of genome resequencing of seven additional horseweed biotypes using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform</span><p id="p-39" class="first-child">R, Resistant; S, susceptible.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T7" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/111512/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/111512/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table VII.</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary of genomic variation among the sequenced horseweed biotypes</span><p id="p-40" class="first-child">For each biotype, genomic DNA was isolated from six individual plants. Variants having frequencies of 90% or greater were considered to be homozygous, whereas variants having frequencies between 20% and 90% were considered to be heterozygous. MNV, Multiple-nucleotide variation; SNV, single-nucleotide variation. *Value of average frequency.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Summary of genome variants and distribution among biotypes. [See online article for color version of this figure.]" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-849132570" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;Summary of genome variants and distribution among biotypes.
