[See online article for color version of this figure.]&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="346"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="346"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/111474" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4.</span> <p id="p-41" class="first-child">Summary of genome variants and distribution among biotypes. A, Total variant counts in different biotypes. B, Percentage of heterozygous and homozygous variants among biotypes. C, Distribution of variant types. Del, Deletion; Ins, insertion; MNV, multiple-nucleotide variation; Rep, replacement; SNV, single-nucleotide variation. D, Number of specific variants in glyphosate-resistant (GR) and glyphosate-susceptible (GS) populations. [See online article for color version of this figure.]</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-42">Phylogenetic analysis of whole-genome <a id="xref-def-7-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a> variation provided a phylogeographic hypothesis regarding the evolution of glyphosate resistance and its spread (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). Biotypes from proximate locations generally shared the same clades, such as the Delaware pairs and the Indiana pairs, which is consistent with our previous studies (<a id="xref-ref-85-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Yuan et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Okada et al., 2013</a>). Although not in the same clade, the TN-R branch was proximate to TN-S. The position of CA-S on the phylogram was basal, which might be of biological importance with regard to gene flow in the species. The phylogenetic pattern suggests that glyphosate resistance in horseweed has evolved independently multiple times (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic tree of sequenced horseweed biotypes based on whole-genome single-nucleotide variation profiles using the shrunk-genomes method of the program PhyloSNP (Faison et al., 2014) with a position delta of zero. Bootstrap supports were all 100%, from 1,000 iterations. The tree was built by using the neighbor-joining method in Phylip 3.695. The scale bar indicates the number of genetic changes per unit of length." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-849132570" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic tree of sequenced horseweed biotypes based on whole-genome single-nucleotide variation profiles using the shrunk-genomes method of the program PhyloSNP (Faison et al., 2014) with a position delta of zero. Bootstrap supports were all 100%, from 1,000 iterations. The tree was built by using the neighbor-joining method in Phylip 3.695. The scale bar indicates the number of genetic changes per unit of length.&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="209"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="209"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/166/3/1241/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/111713" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-43" class="first-child">Phylogenetic tree of sequenced horseweed biotypes based on whole-genome single-nucleotide variation profiles using the shrunk-genomes method of the program PhyloSNP (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Faison et al., 2014</a>) with a position delta of zero. Bootstrap supports were all 100%, from 1,000 iterations. The tree was built by using the neighbor-joining method in Phylip 3.695. The scale bar indicates the number of genetic changes per unit of length.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>5-Enolpyruvylshikimate-3-Phosphate Synthase Genes</h3><p id="p-44">In the horseweed genome, there are two 5-enolpyruvylshikimate-3-phosphate synthase (EPSPS) gene copies (<em>EPSPS1</em> and <em>EPSPS2</em>); thus, no gene amplification was observed. Eleven EPSPS1 and 32 EPSPS2 variants were detected (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S15</a>). However, no EPSPS mutation cosegregated with glyphosate resistance in our data. Furthermore, none of the observed EPSPS sequence variants have been previously reported to be associated with resistance. There also were no significant differences in EPSPS gene expression level among these eight biotypes in response to glyphosate treatment, based on an RNAseq study (Y. Peng, Y. Sang, S. Allen, and C.N. Stewart, unpublished data). Therefore, we conclude that glyphosate resistance in horseweed is conferred by a non-target-site mechanism.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2>DISCUSSION</h2><p id="p-45">To our knowledge, horseweed is the first economically important weed to have its genome sequenced and assembled. The de novo strategy using whole-genome shotgun sequencing in this study was similar to those used in several recent reports (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Al-Dous et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-78-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Wang et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-76-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Varshney et al., 2013</a>), except that we also included third-generation sequencing reads in our study. The 92.3% coverage of 13,996 scaffolds should be considered to be a draft genome comparable to rice, soybean, maize, chickpea, grape, poplar, and date palm (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Goff et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-84-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">Yu et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-73-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Tuskan et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Jaillon et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-63-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Schnable et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-62-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Schmutz et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Al-Dous et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-76-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Varshney et al., 2013</a>). Further improvements in assembly accuracy and backfilling will require the use of either long-insertion mate-pair reads or physical maps (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">International Rice Genome Sequencing Project, 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-78-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Wang et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Al-Mssallem et al., 2013</a>).</p><p id="p-46">The draft horseweed genome has proven to be immediately useful. We have successfully cloned 10 target promoters of interest to study glyphosate resistance and response (data not shown). Moreover, 12 <a id="xref-def-2-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> loci have already been tested and successfully used in a recent horseweed population genetics study (<a id="xref-ref-51-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Okada et al., 2013</a>). Also, the genome sequences have been used to annotate the explosive composition B (hexahydro-1,3,5-trinitro-1,3,5-triazine and 2,4,6-trinitrotoluene) response genes in <em>Baccharis halimifolia</em> (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Ali et al., 2014</a>). Furthermore, we observed that the horseweed chloroplast genome is nearly identical to those of sunflower and lettuce (<a href="https://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu">https://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu</a>). Higher plants have various mitochondrial genome architectures with respect to size and construction compared with the chloroplast genome, which is more conserved (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Tian et al., 2006</a>). <em>Brassica carinata</em> has a small mitochondrial genome (232,241 bp), whereas <em>Cucurbita pepo</em> has a much larger chloroplast genome (982,832 bp; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Table S4</a>).</p><p id="p-47">Next-generation sequencing technologies have been widely used in whole-genome sequencing and resequencing, which has led to the development of rapid genome-wide <a id="xref-def-7-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a> detection applications in model and crop plant species for exploring within-species diversity, genotyping by sequencing, construction of haplotype maps, and performing genome-wide association studies of interesting traits (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Craig et al., 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Huang et al., 2009</a>, <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Atwell et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Todesco et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">Wu et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Kump et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Deschamps et al., 2012</a>). With the reported horseweed genome data, 51,892 <a id="xref-def-2-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> markers and over 2.7 million <a id="xref-def-7-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a>s, as well as chloroplast DNA markers, were identified. Although glyphosate-susceptible and glyphosate-resistant plants, obviously, had opposite phenotypic responses to glyphosate treatment, 99.93% of their genomes were identical. We found many <a id="xref-def-7-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a>s in glyphosate-resistant or glyphosate-susceptible genomes that were located in hot pots, which were defined as those with low <em>P</em> values (<em>P</em> ≤ 0.01), and might be associated with glyphosate resistance. However, many <a id="xref-def-7-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a>s will be silent at the protein sequence level and not of interest for further study in terms of biological functions. Other mutations involved in gene expression regulation (e.g. within promoter regions) are of potential interest for elucidating resistance and other traits. Interestingly, horseweed exhibited considerable morphological variability among accessions (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S13</a>); thus; integrating genomic information with resistance phenomena could be useful in weed science not only in studying the rapid evolution of herbicide resistance but also other traits.</p><p id="p-48">The evolution of glyphosate resistance is a critical process that, in most cases, has been elucidated with regard to genomics and molecular mechanisms. For over a decade, we have known that glyphosate resistance in horseweed is a semidominant trait that is determined by a single simple Mendelian locus (<a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Zelaya et al., 2004</a>). The major focus of weed scientists to combat resistance has been to explore more herbicide control strategies while essentially ignoring the genomic and evolutionary bases of resistance. With few genomic resources for weeds and little expertise to utilize the available resources among the weed science community (<a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Stewart, 2009</a>), discovering the molecular mechanisms underlying nontarget resistance has been extremely difficult. However, now that the genomics era has found its way to weed science, we can begin to answer fundamental questions about what makes weeds so weedy and capable of adapting to control measures and, thereby, design approaches that reduce the further evolution of herbicide resistance in weeds (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Basu et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>). The currently known mechanisms of glyphosate resistance in weedy plants include EPSPS target-site mutations (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Baerson et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Collavo and Sattin, 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Sammons and Gaines, 2014</a>), EPSPS gene amplification (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Gaines et al., 2010</a>, <a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Jugulam et al., 2014</a>), and vacuolar sequestration of glyphosate (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Ge et al., 2010</a>, <a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">2012</a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">2014</a>); however, in the last case, the genes conferring resistance have not been identified.</p><p id="p-49">Non-target-site resistance, especially via altered sequestration, is interesting both biologically and practically for weed management, but is not well characterized at the genomic or molecular level for most weeds (<a id="xref-ref-86-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Yuan et al., 2007</a>). This is the case with the many biotypes of glyphosate-resistant horseweed as well as several other glyphosate-resistant weedy plant species, such as johnsongrass (<em>Sorghum halepense</em>), ryegrass (<em>Lolium</em> spp.), velvet bean (<em>Mucuna pruriens</em>), and wild radish (<em>Raphanus raphanistrum</em>; <a id="xref-ref-50-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Mueller et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Feng et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Main et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-87-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Zelaya et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Koger and Reddy, 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Owen and Zelaya 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Preston and Wakelin, 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Ge et al., 2010</a>, <a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Riar et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Rojano-Delgado et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Vila-Aiub et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Ashworth et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Sammons and Gaines, 2014</a>). Physiologically, we know that non-target-site resistance can involve a plethora of metabolic, conversion, sequestration, and reduced translocation processes, including oxidation, conjugation, or compartmentation of the herbicide molecules (<a id="xref-ref-86-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Yuan et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Cummins et al., 2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Iwakami et al., 2014</a>). Horseweed represents a model weedy plant with a non-target-site glyphosate-resistant mechanism via altered translocation or transport (<a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Feng et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Ge et al., 2010</a>).</p><p id="p-50">An important goal of effective weed management is to stop or slow the evolution and spread of herbicide resistance in weeds. Thus, it is vital to understand whether glyphosate resistance originated once and spread from a single source horseweed population or originated multiple independent times within distinct populations. If resistance originated once and spread, say, via seeds, then the molecular mechanisms would be expected to be the same or similar among populations because of identical descent. In that case, the prevention of seed dispersal and movement by machinery or other means would be an effective resistance management strategy. Alternatively, if resistance originated multiple times and spread from multiple sources, reduction in both seed dispersal and selection pressure will be needed. Management would also benefit from understanding evolutionary dynamics. In the case of horseweed, the evolution of glyphosate resistance occurred independently in multiple locations and might be caused by more than one nontarget resistance gene (<a id="xref-ref-85-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Yuan et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-51-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Okada et al., 2013</a>; this study). Moreover, approximate Bayesian computation (<a id="xref-def-6-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ABC</a>) analyses of microsatellite marker variation indicated that resistant populations underwent expansion after greatly increased glyphosate use in California in the 1990s, but many years before it was detected, strongly suggesting that diversity in weed-control practices prior to herbicide regulation probably kept resistance frequencies low (<a id="xref-ref-51-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Okada et al., 2013</a>). Data from this study also seem not to support the long-range rapid spread of resistance from a single source. At one time, the application of a combination of herbicides simultaneously or in sequence was considered to be an effective resistance management strategy. However, resistance to multiple herbicides is becoming increasingly common (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Ashworth et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Heap, 2014</a>), and this approach is no longer consistently effective. Herbicides with new modes of action or more diversified control measures are desperately needed. Understanding the genomic mechanisms underlying nontarget resistance would allow the development of novel approaches, such as allelopathy and the expanded use of safeners.</p><p id="p-51">For the first time, we have genomics data to begin to address the genetic basis of weediness. Do weeds have unique genes that endow weediness or merely variants of genes common to all plants? Most researchers have posited that the latter situation is most plausible (<a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Basu et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>). Indeed, our finding lends credence to the gene family expansion hypothesis, given that nontarget resistance candidate gene families, <a id="xref-def-6-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ABC</a> transporters, and cytochrome P450 genes, and to a lesser extent <a id="xref-def-4-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GST</a> and <a id="xref-def-5-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GT</a> genes, were overrepresented in horseweed relative to other published plant genomes. Weeds, at least some of them, might have an inordinate adeptness for rapid evolution that is manifested by herbicide resistance; gene family expansion and/or changes in gene expression might be why weeds are weeds (<a id="xref-ref-86-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Yuan et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Shaner, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Ge et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-55-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Peng et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Gaines et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Sammons and Gaines, 2014</a>). Other genes of interest with enriched <a id="xref-def-3-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms, such as vacuole transport, photosynthetic acclimation, and detoxification of nitrogenous compounds, were found (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). Finally, horseweed seems to have relatively more protein-coding genes (44,592; also supported by EST data) in comparison with other sequenced plant genomes while maintaining the smallest genome of all economically important weeds (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Sterck et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Paterson et al., 2010</a>). However, de novo genome assembly (contigs or scaffolds) tends to underestimate repeat numbers and overestimate gene numbers, because repeats are compacted on each other and genes are somewhat fragmentary and get annotated as two unique genes in some cases (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Chaisson and Pevzner, 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Baker, 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Seabury et al., 2013</a>). Repeat numbers in the horseweed genome are lower than in most other sequenced plant genomes, in part because horseweed has a relatively compact genome. However, read-mapping analyses revealed that the read depth of repeated regions was much higher than for nonrepeats. This finding implies that repeat numbers are underestimated in the horseweed genome and that a de novo repeat-searching model will be needed for further analysis.</p><p id="p-52">The small genome size of horseweed and other economically important agricultural weeds might have a practical and evolutionary significance. Twenty-three of the 25 most-studied weedy plant species have 1C genome sizes of less than 5,000 Mb, and 13 of these have genome sizes of less than 2,000 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-69-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Stewart et al., 2009</a>). Recently, a meta-analysis was performed in which invasiveness among plant species was negatively associated with genome size and positively associated with chromosome number (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Pandit et al., 2014</a>). This study analyzed 890 species among 62 genera. Even though it represents one datum, horseweed appears to be, potentially, an archetypical weed in that it has a very streamlined genome but with a large number of genes, which could give it the capacity for rapid evolution in changing environments. Thus, beyond its use for practical agricultural research, the horseweed genome might shed light on standing questions of invasion, evolution, and changing climates (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Caplat et al., 2013</a>). The bane of farmers could be the harbinger of genomic enlightenment.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-8"><h2>MATERIALS AND METHODS</h2><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Plants and Phenotyping</h3><p id="p-53">Seeds sampled from horseweed (<em>Conyza canadensis</em>) populations were germinated and grown in potting medium (3B potting medium, 10-cm-diameter pot, one plant per pot; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S13</a>) in a greenhouse under a 16-/8-h light/dark photoperiod at ambient temperatures (25°C ± 2°C). Twenty-four 3-month-old plants from each population were treated with glyphosate at the field rate (0.84 kg ha<sup>−1</sup>; Roundup Weathermax; Monsanto). Treatment occurred at the rosette stage when plants were 6 to 8 cm in diameter, and glyphosate-treated plants were considered to be resistant if they were alive at the end of 3 weeks (<a id="xref-ref-85-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Yuan et al., 2010</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>DNA Isolation and Genome Sequencing Using Multiple Platforms</h3><p id="p-54">Genomic DNA was extracted from six individual plants of the glyphosate-resistant biotype that was collected originally from a soybean field in Jackson, TN (TN-R), using Plant DNeasy kits (Qiagen). Whole-genome shotgun sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq 2000, 454 GS-FLX Titanium, and PacBio RS sequencing platforms. A total of three sequencing libraries were constructed with insert sizes of approximately 600 bp for the 454 GS-FLX Titanium system. Two paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes of approximately 350 bp and one mate-pair library with insert sizes of approximately 3,000 bp was constructed for the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. One library with insert sizes of approximately 10,000 bp was constructed for the PacBio RS sequencing system.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>De Novo Genome Assembly</h3><p id="p-55">The de novo genome assembly strategy is shown in <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.114.247668/DC1">Supplemental Figure S1</a>. The 454 GS-FLX reads were assembled with Newbler. The Illumina paired-end sequence reads were divided by sequencing depth (approximately 50×) using error-corrected PacBio long reads as guidance (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Koren et al., 2012</a>), and subsets were assembled using SOAPdenovo and CLC Genomic Workbench. NGen was used to combine the primary assembled contigs. CLC Genomic Workbench was used to find the true mate pairs by mapping with the total contigs. NGen was used for the final scaffold construction and gap closure. All the parameter settings were described previously (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Varshney et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-78-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Wang et al., 2012</a>). To check the completeness of the assembly, we mapped ESTs and transcriptome data to the genome assembly using BLASTN (with e value cutoff of 10<sup>−20</sup>).</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Assembly of Chloroplast and Mitochondrial Genomes</h3><p id="p-56">The sequencing reads were screened against custom plant chloroplast and mitochondrial genome databases. The subset sequences for chloroplast and mitochondria were assembled respectively. The complete chloroplast genome was annotated using DOGMA (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Wyman et al., 2004</a>). To remove nonmitochondrial assembly, the mitochondrial contigs were further screened against plant mitochondrial genome databases using BLASTN with e value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Gene and Repeat Annotation</h3><p id="p-57">Gene prediction was performed using homology-based and transcript-based methods. Previous horseweed ESTs and transcriptome data were aligned to the genome assembly using BLAT (blat-34; identity ≥ 0.98, coverage ≥ 0.98) to generate spliced alignments (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Kent, 2002</a>), which were linked according to their overlap using PASA (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Haas et al., 2003</a>). Plant proteins of other species were also mapped to the genome using TBLASTN with e value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>. Predicted transcripts were assigned biological information by searching the UniProt protein database using BLASTX with e value cutoff of 10<sup>−3</sup>. RepeatMasker version 4.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) was used to search putative transposable element regions of the horseweed genome against an updated set of RepeatMasker libraries (20140131). To develop <a id="xref-def-2-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> markers for further population genetic studies, the masked <a id="xref-def-2-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">SSR</a> loci were further analyzed using the Simple Sequence Repeat Identification Tool (<a href="http://archive.gramene.org/db/markers/ssrtool">http://archive.gramene.org/db/markers/ssrtool</a>) with a threshold minimum of six repeats.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3><a id="xref-def-3-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> Annotation and Enrichment Analysis</h3><p id="p-58">Results of BLASTX of horseweed transcripts were further mapped using the <a id="xref-def-3-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> database to assign <a id="xref-def-3-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> terms and carry out <a id="xref-def-3-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">GO</a> annotation using the Blast2Go program integrated in CLC Genomic Workbench (<a href="http://www.blast2go.com">http://www.blast2go.com</a>). Enrichment analysis was performed by using the entire set of available Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>) transcripts as a reference. The <em>P</em> value of Fisher’s exact test was set to <em>P</em> ≤ 0.001 to reduce noise and output the most specific terms.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Resequencing and Variation Analysis</h3><p id="p-59">DNA samples of another seven horseweed biotypes were isolated using the method described above. Paired-end DNA libraries (approximately 350-bp insertion) were constructed for each biotype, bar coded, and subjected to Illumina sequencing using the HiSeq 2000 platform. After trimming and quality-control steps, the remaining data for each population were assembled into genomes with the TN-R assembly as reference using CLC Genomic Workbench 7.03. The probabilistic variant detection model was chosen to report the variants. A minimum cutoff value (20 times or greater coverage at the variant loci, 20% or greater frequency) was applied to avoid issues of sequencing and assembly errors in variant calling. Fisher’s exact test was carried out to detect specific variants in glyphosate-susceptible or glyphosate-resistant groups. The cutoff sample frequency was 90% or greater, which meant that only variants present in all test samples, but not in any reference samples, would be reported. To carry out phylogenetic analysis based on whole-genome single-nucleotide variation profiles among sequenced horseweed biotypes, the shrunk-genomes method of the program PhyloSNP (<a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Faison et al., 2014</a>) with a position delta of zero surrounding each <a id="xref-def-7-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a> was chosen. The algorithm of a quantitative method was used, in which the created matrix of presence/absence and the position of each <a id="xref-def-7-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">SNP</a> from the shrunk-genome alignment can be used directly to generate a distance matrix among the biotypes. Finally, the tree was built by using the neighbor-joining method in Phylip 3.695.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Supplemental Data</h3><p id="p-60">The following materials are available in the online version of this article.</p><ul class="list-simple " id="list-1"><li id="list-item-1"><p id="p-61"><strong><a
